<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Bread</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Food made of flour and water</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Bread_(disambiguation)" title="Bread (disambiguation)">Bread (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox hrecipe adr"><caption class="infobox-title fn"><span>Bread</span></caption><tbody><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korb_mit_Br%C3%B6tchen.JPG"><img alt="Loaves of bread in a basket" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Korb_mit_Br%C3%B6tchen.JPG/250px-Korb_mit_Br%C3%B6tchen.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Korb_mit_Br%C3%B6tchen.JPG/375px-Korb_mit_Br%C3%B6tchen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Korb_mit_Br%C3%B6tchen.JPG/500px-Korb_mit_Br%C3%B6tchen.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a><div class="infobox-caption" style="padding-bottom:0.25em;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">Various leavened breads</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="line-height:1.15em; padding-right:0.65em;">Main ingredients</th><td class="infobox-data ingredient"><a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">Flour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.25em;"><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/16px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/24px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/32px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Bread" title="b:Cookbook:Bread">Cookbook: Bread</a></li><li> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/18px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/24px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Bread" title="commons:Bread">Media: Bread</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Bread</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> prepared from a <a href="/wiki/Dough" title="Dough">dough</a> of <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">flour</a> (usually <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>) and water, usually by <a href="/wiki/Baking" title="Baking">baking</a>. Throughout <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a> and around the world, it has been an important part of many cultures' diet. It is one of the oldest human-made foods, having been of significance since the dawn of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, and plays an essential role in both religious rituals and secular culture.
</p><p>Bread may be <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">leavened</a> by naturally occurring microbes (e.g. sourdough), chemicals (e.g. baking soda), industrially produced <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker's yeast">yeast</a>, or high-pressure aeration, which creates the gas bubbles that fluff up bread. In many countries, commercial bread often contains additives to improve flavor, texture, color, shelf life, nutrition, and ease of production.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_bread" title="History of bread">History of bread</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:7-alimenti,_pane,_Taccuino_Sanitatis,_Casanatense_4182.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1075" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/7-alimenti%2C_pane%2C_Taccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182.jpg/220px-7-alimenti%2C_pane%2C_Taccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/7-alimenti%2C_pane%2C_Taccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182.jpg/330px-7-alimenti%2C_pane%2C_Taccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/7-alimenti%2C_pane%2C_Taccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182.jpg/440px-7-alimenti%2C_pane%2C_Taccuino_Sanitatis%2C_Casanatense_4182.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:7-alimenti,_pane,_Taccuino_Sanitatis,_Casanatense_4182.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bread shop, <i><a href="/wiki/Tacuinum_Sanitatis" title="Tacuinum Sanitatis">Tacuinum Sanitatis</a></i> from Northern Italy, beginning of the 15th century</div></div></div>
<p>Bread is one of the oldest prepared foods. Evidence from 30,000 years ago in Europe and Australia revealed starch residue on rocks used for pounding plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It is possible that during this time, starch extract from the roots of plants, such as cattails and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferns" title="Ferns">ferns</a>, was spread on a flat rock, placed over a fire and cooked into a primitive form of <a href="/wiki/Flatbread" title="Flatbread">flatbread</a>. The world's oldest evidence of bread-making has been found in a 14,500-year-old <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian</a> site in Jordan's northeastern desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Around 10,000 BC, with the dawn of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> age and the spread of agriculture, grains became the mainstay of making bread. Yeast spores are ubiquitous, including on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal grains</a>, so any dough left to rest leavens naturally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>An early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leavened_bread" title="Leavened bread">leavened bread</a> was baked as early as 6000 BC in southern Mesopotamia, cradle of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumerians" title="Sumerians">Sumerian</a> civilization, who may have passed on the knowledge to the Egyptians around 3000 BC. The Egyptians refined the process and started adding <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">flour</a>. The Sumerians were already using <a href="/wiki/Ash" title="Ash">ash</a> to supplement the dough as it was baked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arzani11_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-arzani11-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>There were multiple sources of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leavening" title="Leavening">leavening</a> available for early bread. Airborne yeasts could be harnessed by leaving uncooked dough exposed to air for some time before cooking. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> reported that the <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iberians" title="Iberians">Iberians</a> used the foam skimmed from <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Barm" title="Barm">barm</a>, to produce "a lighter kind of bread than other peoples" such as <a href="/wiki/Barm_cake" title="Barm cake">barm cake</a>. Parts of the ancient world that drank wine instead of beer used a paste composed of <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a> juice and flour that was allowed to begin fermenting, or wheat bran steeped in <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>, as a source for <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>. The most common source of leavening was to retain a piece of dough from the previous day to use as a form of sourdough <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bread_starter" title="Bread starter">starter</a>, as Pliny also reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancient Egyptians, Greeks, and Romans all considered the degree of refinement in the bakery arts as a sign of civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arzani11_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-arzani11-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chorleywood_bread_process" title="Chorleywood bread process">Chorleywood bread process</a> was developed in 1961; it uses the intense mechanical working of dough to dramatically reduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> period and the time taken to produce a loaf. The process, whose high-energy mixing allows for the use of grain with a lower protein content, is now widely used around the world in large factories. As a result, bread can be produced very quickly and at low costs to the manufacturer and the consumer. However, there has been some criticism of the effect on nutritional value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_breads" title="List of breads">List of breads</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:131px;max-width:131px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:96px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Breadindia.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Breadindia.jpg/129px-Breadindia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Breadindia.jpg/194px-Breadindia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Breadindia.jpg/258px-Breadindia.jpg 2x" width="129"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Brown bread (left) and whole grain bread</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:99px;max-width:99px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:96px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Essene_Bread_Spelt_Sproud_cut.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1319" data-file-width="1321" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Essene_Bread_Spelt_Sproud_cut.JPG/97px-Essene_Bread_Spelt_Sproud_cut.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Essene_Bread_Spelt_Sproud_cut.JPG/146px-Essene_Bread_Spelt_Sproud_cut.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Essene_Bread_Spelt_Sproud_cut.JPG/194px-Essene_Bread_Spelt_Sproud_cut.JPG 2x" width="97"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Dark <a href="/wiki/Sprouted_bread" title="Sprouted bread">sprouted bread</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:106px;max-width:106px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:96px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="1465" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg/104px-Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg/156px-Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg/208px-Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg 2x" width="104"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Ruisreik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4" title="Ruisreikäleipä">Ruisreikäleipä</a>, a flat <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> flour loaf with a hole</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Bread is the <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and in European-derived cultures such as those in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>. This is in contrast to parts of South and East Asia, where <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noodles" title="Noodles">noodles</a> are the staple. Bread is usually made from a <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>-<a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">flour</a> <a href="/wiki/Dough" title="Dough">dough</a> that is cultured with yeast, allowed to rise, and finally baked in an <a href="/wiki/Oven" title="Oven">oven</a>. The addition of yeast to the bread explains the air pockets commonly found in bread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Owing to its high levels of <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> (which give the dough sponginess and elasticity), <a href="/wiki/Common_wheat" title="Common wheat">common or bread wheat</a> is the most common grain used for the preparation of bread, which makes the largest single contribution to the world's food supply of any food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sangak_17.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Sangak_17.jpg/220px-Sangak_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Sangak_17.jpg/330px-Sangak_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Sangak_17.jpg/440px-Sangak_17.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sangak_17.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sangak" title="Sangak">Sangak</a>, an Iranian flatbread</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg/220px-Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg/330px-Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg/440px-Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Strucia — a type of European sweet bread</div></div></div>
<p>Bread is also made from the flour of other wheat species (including <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einkorn" title="Einkorn">einkorn</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamut" title="Kamut">kamut</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Non-wheat cereals including <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (corn), <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">oats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> have been used to make bread, but, with the exception of rye, usually in combination with wheat flour as they have less gluten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gluten-free_diet#Gluten-free_bread" title="Gluten-free diet">Gluten-free breads</a> are made using flours from a variety of ingredients such as almonds, rice, sorghum, corn, legumes such as beans, and tubers such as cassava. Since these foods lack gluten, dough made from them may not hold its shape as the loaves rise, and their crumb may be dense with little aeration. Additives such as <a href="/wiki/Xanthan_gum" title="Xanthan gum">xanthan gum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guar_gum" title="Guar gum">guar gum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxypropyl_methylcellulose" title="Hydroxypropyl methylcellulose">hydroxypropyl methylcellulose</a> (HPMC), <a href="/wiki/Corn_starch" title="Corn starch">corn starch</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Egg_as_food" title="Egg as food">eggs</a> are used to compensate for the lack of gluten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LamacchiaCamarca2014_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-LamacchiaCamarca2014-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VoltaCaio2015Quotation_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-VoltaCaio2015Quotation-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MulderWanrooijQuotation_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-MulderWanrooijQuotation-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HischenhuberCrevelQuotation_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-HischenhuberCrevelQuotation-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical-chemical_composition">Physical-chemical composition</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_phenol" title="Natural phenol">phenolic</a> compounds are mainly found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hull_(botany)" title="Hull (botany)">hulls</a> in the form of insoluble bound <a href="/wiki/Ferulic_acid" title="Ferulic acid">ferulic acid</a>, where it is relevant to wheat resistance to fungal diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rye_bread" title="Rye bread">Rye bread</a> contains <a href="/wiki/Phenolic_acid" title="Phenolic acid">phenolic acids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferulic_acid_dehydrodimer" title="Ferulic acid dehydrodimer">ferulic acid dehydrodimers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_phenol" title="Natural phenol">natural phenolic</a> glucosides, <a href="/wiki/Secoisolariciresinol_diglucoside" title="Secoisolariciresinol diglucoside">secoisolariciresinol diglucoside</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-coumaric_acid_glucoside" title="P-coumaric acid glucoside">p-coumaric acid glucoside</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ferulic_acid_glucoside&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ferulic acid glucoside (page does not exist)">ferulic acid glucoside</a>, can be found in commercial breads containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flaxseed" title="Flaxseed">flaxseed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg/220px-Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg/330px-Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg/440px-Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Small home made bread with <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin_seed" title="Pumpkin seed">pumpkin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunflower_seed" title="Sunflower seed">sunflower seeds</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Glutenin" title="Glutenin">Glutenin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gliadin" title="Gliadin">gliadin</a> are functional proteins found in wheat bread that contribute to the structure of bread. Glutenin forms interconnected gluten networks within bread through interchain disulfide bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wieser_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wieser-23">[23]</a></sup> Gliadin binds weakly to the gluten network established by glutenin via intrachain disulfide bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wieser_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wieser-23">[23]</a></sup> Structurally, bread can be defined as an elastic-plastic <a href="/wiki/Foam" title="Foam">foam</a> (same as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expanded_polystyrene" title="Expanded polystyrene">styrofoam</a>). The glutenin protein contributes to its <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elastic</a> nature, as it is able to regain its initial shape after deformation. The gliadin protein contributes to its <a href="/wiki/Plasticity_(physics)" title="Plasticity (physics)">plastic</a> nature, because it demonstrates non-reversible structural change after a certain amount of applied force. Because air pockets within this gluten network result from carbon dioxide production during leavening, bread can be defined as a foam, or a gas-in-solid solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Acrylamide" title="Acrylamide">Acrylamide</a>, like in other starchy foods that have been heated higher than 120 °C (248 °F), has been found in recent years to occur in bread. Acrylamide is <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxicity" title="Neurotoxicity">neurotoxic</a>, has adverse effects on male reproduction and developmental toxicity and is <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogenic</a>. A study has found that more than 99 percent of the acrylamide in bread is found in the crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hohenheim" title="University of Hohenheim">University of Hohenheim</a> found that industrially produced bread typically has a high proportion of <a href="/wiki/FODMAP" title="FODMAP">FODMAP</a> carbohydrates due to a short rising time (often only one hour). The high proportion of FODMAP carbohydrates in such bread then causes <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a>. This is particularly problematic in intestinal diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Irritable_bowel_syndrome" title="Irritable bowel syndrome">irritable bowel syndrome</a>. While in traditional bread making the dough rises for several hours, industrial breads rise for a much shorter time, usually only one hour. However, a sufficiently long rising time is important to break down the indigestible FODMAP carbohydrates. Some flours (for example, <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Einkorn_wheat" title="Einkorn wheat">einkorn</a>) contain fewer FODMAPs, but the difference between grain types is relatively small (between 1 and 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weight_percent" title="Weight percent">percent by weight</a>). Instead, 90% of the FODMAPs that cause discomfort can be broken down during a rising time of 4 hours. In the study, whole-grain yeast doughs were examined after different rising times; the highest level of FODMAPs was present after one hour in each case and decreased thereafter. The study thus shows that it is essentially the baking technique and not the type of grain that determines whether a bread is well tolerated or not. A better tolerance of bread made from original cereals can therefore not be explained by the original cereal itself, but rather by the fact that traditional, artisanal baking techniques are generally used when baking original cereals, which include a long dough process. The study also showed that a long rising time also breaks down undesirable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytates" title="Phytates">phytates</a> more effectively, flavors develop better, and the finished bread contains more biologically accessible <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace elements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary_uses">Culinary uses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1391" data-file-width="1945" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG/220px-Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG/330px-Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG/440px-Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bread_pudding" title="Bread pudding">Bread pudding</a></div></div></div>
<p>Bread can be served at many <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperatures</a>; once baked, it can subsequently be <a href="/wiki/Toast_(food)" title="Toast (food)">toasted</a>.  It is most commonly eaten with the hands, either by itself or as a carrier for other foods. Bread can be spread with <a href="/wiki/Butter" title="Butter">butter</a>, dipped into liquids such as <a href="/wiki/Gravy" title="Gravy">gravy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Soup" title="Soup">soup</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> it can be topped with various sweet and savory spreads, or used to make <a href="/wiki/Sandwich" title="Sandwich">sandwiches</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meats</a>, cheeses, vegetables, and  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condiments" title="Condiments">condiments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bread is used as an ingredient in other culinary preparations, such as the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breadcrumb" title="Breadcrumb">breadcrumbs</a> to provide crunchy crusts or thicken sauces; toasted cubes of bread, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Croutons" title="Croutons">croutons</a>, are used as a salad topping; seasoned bread is used as <a href="/wiki/Stuffing" title="Stuffing">stuffing</a> inside roasted turkey; sweet or savoury <a href="/wiki/Bread_pudding" title="Bread pudding">bread puddings</a> are made with bread and various liquids; egg and milk-soaked bread is fried as <a href="/wiki/French_toast" title="French toast">French toast</a>; and bread is used as a binding agent in <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meatball" title="Meatball">meatballs</a> and other ground meat products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutritional_significance">Nutritional significance</span></h3>
<p>Nutritionally, bread is categorized as a source of grains in the <a href="/wiki/Food_pyramid_(nutrition)" title="Food pyramid (nutrition)">food pyramid</a>. Further, it is a good source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> and nutrients such as magnesium, iron, selenium, B vitamins, and dietary fiber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crust">Crust</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg/220px-Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg/330px-Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg/440px-Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roggenvollkornbrot.Endstueck.Kruste.mit.Ausbund.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crust of a cut bread made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whole-grain" title="Whole-grain">whole-grain</a> <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> with crust crack (half right at the top)</div></div></div>
<p>Bread crust is formed from surface dough during the cooking process. It is hardened and browned through the <a href="/wiki/Maillard_reaction" title="Maillard reaction">Maillard reaction</a> using the sugars and amino acids due to the intense heat at the bread surface. The crust of most breads is harder, and more complexly and intensely flavored, than the rest. <a href="/wiki/Old_wives%27_tale" title="Old wives' tale">Old wives' tales</a> suggest that eating the bread crust makes a person's hair curlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Additionally, the crust is rumored to be healthier than the remainder of the bread. Some studies have shown that this is true as the crust has more <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antioxidants" title="Antioxidants">antioxidants</a> such as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pronyl-lysine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pronyl-lysine (page does not exist)">pronyl-lysine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation">Preparation</span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Composition_and_chemistry"></span><span class="anchor" id="Chemistry_and_composition"></span>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3194" data-file-width="4252" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg/220px-Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg/330px-Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg/440px-Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steps in bread making, here for an unleavened Chilean tortilla</div></div></div>
<p>Doughs are usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baked" title="Baked">baked</a>, but in some cuisines breads are <a href="/wiki/Steamed_bread" title="Steamed bread">steamed</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Mantou" title="Mantou">mantou</a>), fried (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Puri_(food)" title="Puri (food)">puri</a>), or baked on an unoiled <a href="/wiki/Frying_pan" title="Frying pan">frying pan</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Tortilla" title="Tortilla">tortillas</a>). It may be <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">leavened</a> or unleavened (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matzo" title="Matzo">matzo</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edible_salt" title="Edible salt">Salt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">leavening agents</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeast_(baking)" title="Yeast (baking)">yeast</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baking_soda" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a> are common ingredients, though bread may contain other ingredients, such as <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">egg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">raisins</a>), <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Onion" title="Onion">onion</a>), <a href="/wiki/Nut_(fruit)" title="Nut (fruit)">nuts</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">walnut</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Poppy_seed" title="Poppy seed">poppy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methods of processing dough into bread include the <a href="/wiki/Straight_dough" title="Straight dough">straight dough process</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sourdough" title="Sourdough">sourdough process</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chorleywood_bread_process" title="Chorleywood bread process">Chorleywood bread process</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sponge_and_dough" title="Sponge and dough">sponge and dough process</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg/220px-Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg/330px-Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg/440px-Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baking bread in <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formulation">Formulation</span></h3>
<p>Professional bread recipes are stated using the <a href="/wiki/Baker_percentage" title="Baker percentage">baker's percentage</a> notation. The amount of flour is denoted to be 100%, and the other ingredients are expressed as a percentage of that amount by weight. Measurement by weight is more accurate and consistent than measurement by volume, particularly for dry ingredients. The proportion of water to flour is the most important measurement in a bread recipe, as it affects texture and crumb the most. Hard wheat flours <a href="/wiki/Farinograph#Method" title="Farinograph">absorb</a> about 62% <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, while softer wheat flours absorb about 56%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Common table breads made from these doughs result in a finely textured, light bread. Most artisan bread formulas contain anywhere from 60 to 75% water. In yeast breads, the higher water percentages result in more CO<sub>2</sub> bubbles and a coarser bread crumb. 500 <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">grams</a> (1 pound) of flour yields a standard loaf of bread or two <a href="/wiki/Baguette" title="Baguette">baguettes</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcium_propionate" title="Calcium propionate">Calcium propionate</a> is commonly added by commercial bakeries to retard the growth of molds.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flour">Flour</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">Flour</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">Flour</a> is grain ground to a powdery consistency. Flour provides the primary structure, starch and protein to the final baked bread. The <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> content of the flour is the best indicator of the quality of the bread <a href="/wiki/Dough" title="Dough">dough</a> and the finished bread. While bread can be made from all-purpose wheat flour, a specialty bread flour, containing more protein (12–14%), is recommended for high-quality bread. If one uses a flour with a lower protein content (9–11%) to produce bread, a shorter mixing time is required to develop gluten strength properly. An extended mixing time leads to oxidization of the dough, which gives the finished product a whiter crumb, instead of the cream color preferred by most artisan bakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wheat flour, in addition to its starch, contains three water-soluble protein groups (<a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">albumin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Globulin" title="Globulin">globulin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Proteose" title="Proteose">proteoses</a>) and two water-insoluble protein groups (<a href="/wiki/Glutenin" title="Glutenin">glutenin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gliadin" title="Gliadin">gliadin</a>). When flour is mixed with water, the water-soluble proteins dissolve, leaving the glutenin and gliadin to form the structure of the resulting bread. When relatively dry dough is worked by <a href="/wiki/Kneading" title="Kneading">kneading</a>, or wet dough is allowed to rise for a long time (see <a href="/wiki/No-knead_bread" title="No-knead bread">no-knead bread</a>), the glutenin forms strands of long, thin, chainlike molecules, while the shorter gliadin forms bridges between the strands of glutenin. The resulting networks of strands produced by these two proteins are known as <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>. Gluten development improves if the dough is allowed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autolyse" title="Autolyse">autolyse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquids">Liquids</span></h3>
<p>Water, or some other liquid, is used to form the flour into a paste or dough. The weight or ratio of liquid required varies between recipes, but a ratio of three parts liquid to five parts flour is common for yeast breads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Recipes that use steam as the primary leavening method may have a liquid content in excess of one part liquid to one part flour. Instead of water, recipes may use liquids such as milk or other <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">dairy products</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">buttermilk</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>), fruit juice, or eggs. These contribute additional sweeteners, fats, or leavening components, as well as water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fats_or_shortenings">Fats or shortenings</span></h3>
<p>Fats, such as butter, vegetable oils, lard, or that contained in eggs, affect the development of gluten in breads by coating and lubricating the individual strands of protein. They also help to hold the structure together. If too much fat is included in a bread dough, the lubrication effect causes the protein structures to divide. A fat content of approximately 3% by weight is the concentration that produces the greatest leavening action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In addition to their effects on leavening, fats also serve to tenderize breads and preserve freshness.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bread_improvers">Bread improvers</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bread_improver" title="Bread improver">Bread improver</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bread_improver" title="Bread improver">Bread improvers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dough_conditioner" title="Dough conditioner">dough conditioners</a> are often used in producing commercial breads to reduce the time needed for rising and to improve texture and volume and to give <a href="/wiki/Staling" title="Staling">antistaling</a> effects. The substances used may be oxidising agents to strengthen the dough or reducing agents to develop gluten and reduce mixing time, emulsifiers to strengthen the dough or to provide other properties such as making slicing easier, or enzymes to increase gas production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tenbergen_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tenbergen-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salt">Salt</span></h3>
<p>Salt (<a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>) is very often added to enhance flavor and restrict yeast activity. It also affects the crumb and the overall texture by stabilizing and strengthening<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> the gluten. Some artisan bakers forego early addition of salt to the dough, whether wholemeal or refined, and wait until after a 20-minute rest to allow the dough to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autolyse" title="Autolyse">autolyse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mixtures of salts are sometimes employed, such as employing <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> to reduce the sodium level, and <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">monosodium glutamate</a> to give flavor (<a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leavening">Leavening</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg/220px-Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg/330px-Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg/440px-Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dough trough, located in <a href="/wiki/Aberdour_Castle" title="Aberdour Castle">Aberdour Castle</a>, once used for leavening bread.</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leavening" title="Leavening">Leavening</a> is the process of adding gas to a dough before or during baking to produce a lighter, more easily chewed bread. Most bread eaten in the West is leavened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAC_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemicals">Chemicals</span></h3>
<p>A simple technique for leavening bread is the use of gas-producing chemicals.  There are two common methods. The first is to use <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-raising_flour" title="Self-raising flour">self-raising flour</a> that includes baking powder.  The second is to include an acidic ingredient such as <a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">buttermilk</a> and add <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baking_soda" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a>; the reaction of the acid with the soda produces gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAC_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-44">[44]</a></sup> Chemically leavened breads are called <i><a href="/wiki/Quick_bread" title="Quick bread">quick breads</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Soda_bread" title="Soda bread">soda breads</a></i>. This method is commonly used to make <a href="/wiki/Muffin" title="Muffin">muffins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancake" title="Pancake">pancakes</a>, American-style <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biscuits" title="Biscuits">biscuits</a>, and quick breads such as <a href="/wiki/Banana_bread" title="Banana bread">banana bread</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yeast">Yeast</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker's yeast">Baker's yeast</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1330" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg/220px-Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg/330px-Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg/440px-Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Compressed fresh yeast</div></div></div>
<p>Many breads are leavened by <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>. The yeast most commonly used for leavening bread is <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i>, the same species used for brewing alcoholic beverages. This yeast <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">ferments</a> some of the <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> in the flour, including any <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. Commercial bakers often leaven their dough with commercially produced <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker's yeast">baker's yeast</a>. Baker's yeast has the advantage of producing uniform, quick, and reliable results, because it is obtained from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pure_culture" title="Pure culture">pure culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAC_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-44">[44]</a></sup> Many artisan bakers produce their own yeast with a growth culture. If kept in the right conditions, it provides leavening for many years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-387-38563-0-cite4_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-387-38563-0-cite4-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The baker's yeast and <a href="/wiki/Sourdough" title="Sourdough">sourdough</a> methods follow the same pattern. Water is mixed with flour, salt and the leavening agent. Other additions (spices, herbs, fats, seeds, fruit, etc.) are not needed to bake bread, but are often used. The mixed dough is then allowed to <a href="/wiki/Proofing_(baking_technique)" title="Proofing (baking technique)">rise</a> one or more times (a longer rising time results in more flavor, so bakers often "punch down" the dough and let it rise again), loaves are formed, and (after an optional final rising time) the bread is baked in an <a href="/wiki/Oven" title="Oven">oven</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAC_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many breads are made from a "<a href="/wiki/Straight_dough" title="Straight dough">straight dough</a>", which means that all of the ingredients are combined in one step, and the dough is baked after the rising time;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAC_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-44">[44]</a></sup> others are made from a "<a href="/wiki/Pre-ferment" title="Pre-ferment">pre-ferment</a>" in which the leavening agent is combined with some of the flour and water a day or so ahead of baking and allowed to ferment overnight. On the day of baking, the rest of the ingredients are added, and the process continues as with straight dough. This produces a more flavorful bread with better texture. Many bakers see the starter method as a compromise between the reliable results of baker's yeast and the flavor and complexity of a longer fermentation. It also allows the baker to use only a minimal amount of baker's yeast, which was scarce and expensive when it first became available. Most yeasted pre-ferments fall into one of three categories: "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poolish" title="Poolish">poolish</a>" or "pouliche", a loose-textured mixture composed of roughly equal amounts of flour and water (by weight); "<a href="/wiki/Biga_(bread_baking)" title="Biga (bread baking)">biga</a>", a stiff mixture with a higher proportion of flour; and "pâte fermentée", which is simply a portion of dough reserved from a previous batch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 126.66666666667px"><div style="width: 126.66666666667px">

</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sourdough">Sourdough</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sourdough" title="Sourdough">Sourdough</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg/220px-Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg/330px-Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg/440px-Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sourdough loaves</div></div></div>
<p>Sourdough is a type of bread produced by a long fermentation of dough using naturally occurring yeasts and <a href="/wiki/Lactobacillus" title="Lactobacillus">lactobacilli</a>. It usually has a mildly sour taste because of the <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> produced during <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> by the lactobacilli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DavidsonSourdough_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonSourdough-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sourdough breads are made with a sourdough starter. The starter cultivates yeast and lactobacilli in a mixture of flour and water, making use of the microorganisms already present on flour; it does not need any added yeast. A starter may be maintained indefinitely by regular additions of flour and water. Some bakers have starters many generations old, which are said to have a special taste or texture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DavidsonSourdough_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonSourdough-48">[48]</a></sup> At one time, all yeast-leavened breads were sourdoughs. Recently there has been a revival of sourdough bread in artisan bakeries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, peasant families throughout Europe baked on a fixed schedule, perhaps once a week. The starter was saved from the previous week's dough. The starter was mixed with the new ingredients, the dough was left to rise, and then a piece of it was saved (to be the starter for next week's bread).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAC_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam">Steam</span></h3>
<p>The rapid expansion of steam produced during baking leavens the bread, which is as simple as it is unpredictable.  Steam-leavening is unpredictable since the steam is not produced until the bread is baked. Steam leavening happens regardless of the raising agents (baking soda, yeast, baking powder, sour dough, beaten egg white) included in the mix. The leavening agent either contains air bubbles or generates carbon dioxide. The heat vaporises the water from the inner surface of the bubbles within the dough. The steam expands and makes the bread rise. This is the main factor in the rising of bread once it has been put in the oven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> generation, on its own, is too small to account for the rise. Heat kills bacteria or yeast at an early stage, so the CO<sub>2</sub> generation is stopped.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacteria">Bacteria</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Salt-rising_bread" title="Salt-rising bread">Salt-rising bread</a> employs a form of <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> leavening that does not require yeast. Although the leavening action is inconsistent, and requires close attention to the <a href="/wiki/Incubator_(culture)" title="Incubator (culture)">incubating</a> conditions, this bread is making a comeback for its cheese-like flavor and fine texture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aeration">Aeration</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerated_bread" title="Aerated bread">Aerated bread</a> was leavened by carbon dioxide being forced into dough under pressure. From the mid-19th to mid-20th centuries, bread made this way was somewhat popular in the United Kingdom, made by the <a href="/wiki/Aerated_Bread_Company" title="Aerated Bread Company">Aerated Bread Company</a> and sold in its high-street <a href="/wiki/Aerated_Bread_Company#Tea_shops_and_early_women's_issues" title="Aerated Bread Company">tearooms</a>. The company was founded in 1862, and ceased independent operations in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-memoir_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-memoir-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Pressure-Vacuum mixer was later developed by the Flour Milling and Baking Research Association for the <a href="/wiki/Chorleywood_bread_process" title="Chorleywood bread process">Chorleywood bread process</a>. It manipulates the gas bubble size and optionally the composition of gases in the dough via the gas applied to the headspace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_English_language" title="Old English language">Old English</a> word for bread was <i>hlaf</i> (<i>hlaifs</i> in <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>: modern English <i><a href="/wiki/Loaf" title="Loaf">loaf</a></i>), which appears to be the oldest <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Teutonic</a> name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-etym-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>hleib</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and modern <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Laib</i> derive from this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> word, which was borrowed into some Slavic (<a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a> <i>chléb</i>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> <i>bochen chleba</i>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <i>khleb</i>) and Finnic (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finnish_(language)" title="Finnish (language)">Finnish</a> <i>leipä</i>, <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> <i>leib</i>) languages as well.
The <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">Modern English</a> word bread appears in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_language" title="Germanic language">Germanic languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/West_Frisian_language" title="West Frisian language">West Frisian</a> <i>brea</i>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i>brood</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Brot</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swedish_(language)" title="Swedish (language)">Swedish</a> <i>bröd</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> <i>brød</i>; it may be related to <i><a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">brew</a></i> or perhaps to <i>break</i>, originally meaning "broken piece", "morsel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (August 2021)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2794" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg/220px-Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg/330px-Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg/440px-Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Ukrainian woman in national dress welcoming with <a href="/wiki/Bread_and_salt" title="Bread and salt">bread and salt</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bread_in_culture" title="Bread in culture">Bread in culture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bread_in_culture" title="Bread in culture">Bread has a significance beyond mere nutrition in many cultures</a> because of its history and contemporary importance. Bread is also <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_bread" title="Sacramental bread">significant in Christianity</a> as one of the elements (alongside <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and in other religions including <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">Paganism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a>, bread is a <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphor</a> for basic necessities and living conditions in general. For example, a "bread-winner" is a household's main economic contributor and has little to do with actual bread-provision. This is also seen in the phrase "putting bread on the table". The Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Juvenal" title="Juvenal">Juvenal</a> satirized superficial politicians and the public as caring only for "<i>panem et circenses</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Bread_and_circuses" title="Bread and circuses">bread and circuses</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Russia</a> in 1917, the Bolsheviks promised "peace, land, and bread."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Breadbasket" title="Breadbasket">breadbasket</a>" denotes an agriculturally productive region. In parts of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a> Europe <a href="/wiki/Bread_and_salt" title="Bread and salt">bread and salt</a> is offered as a welcome to guests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, life's basic necessities are often referred to as "roti, kapra aur makan" (bread, cloth, and house).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Words for bread, including "dough" and "bread" itself, are used in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>-speaking countries as <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonyms</a> for <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">money</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-etym-55">[55]</a></sup> A remarkable or revolutionary innovation may be called the best thing since "<a href="/wiki/Sliced_bread" title="Sliced bread">sliced bread</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The expression "to break bread with someone" means "to share a meal with someone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The English word "lord" comes from the Anglo-Saxon <i>hlāfweard</i>, meaning "bread keeper."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bread is sometimes referred to as "the staff of life", although this term can refer to other staple foods in different cultures: the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> defines it as "bread (or similar staple food)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-camb_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-camb-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-69">[69]</a></sup> This is sometimes thought to be a biblical reference, but the nearest wording is in Leviticus 26 "when I have broken the staff of your bread".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levit_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-levit-70">[70]</a></sup> The term has been adopted in the names of bakery firms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kendal_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-kendal-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>

Woodhead. p. 448. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-85573-724-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-85573-724-2"><bdi>978-1-85573-724-2</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Texture+in+food&amp;rft.pages=448&amp;rft.pub=Woodhead&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-85573-724-2&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Du-K8UuyKT48C%26pg%3DPA448&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABread"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-etym-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-etym_55-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-etym_55-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFHarper">Harper, Douglas. <a class="external text" href="https://www.etymonline.com/?term=bread" rel="nofollow">"bread"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Online_Etymology_Dictionary" title="Online Etymology Dictionary">Online Etymology Dictionary</a></i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Online+Etymology+Dictionary&amp;rft.atitle=bread&amp;rft.aulast=Harper&amp;rft.aufirst=Douglas&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.etymonline.com%2F%3Fterm%3Dbread&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABread"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDiakonov1999">Diakonov, Igor M. (1999). <i>The Paths of History</i>. Cambridge University Press. p. 79. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-64398-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-64398-6"><bdi>978-0-521-64398-6</bdi></a>. <q>Slavic langues retain many Gothic words, reflecting cultural borrowings: thus <i>khleb</i>, (bread) from an earlier <i>khleiba</i> from Gothic <i>hlaifs</i>, or, rather, from the more ancient form <i>hlaibhaz</i>, which meant bread baked in an oven (and, probably, made with yeast), as different from a l-iepekha, which was a flat cake moulded (liepiti) from paste, and baked on charcoal. [the same nominal stem *hlaibh- has been preserved in modern English as loaf; cf. Lord, from ancient <i>hlafweard</i> bread-keeper]</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Paths+of+History&amp;rft.pages=79&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-64398-6&amp;rft.aulast=Diakonov&amp;rft.aufirst=Igor+M.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABread"></span></span>
</li>
